\begin{center}{\bf Selective glossary}\end{center}\par
\newpage
\begin{center}{\bf Preface}\end{center}\par

I need not discuss the grammatical idiosyncrasies of the Niryuktis and
Bha1s2yas the composers of which resemble Procrustes in that they not
only cause words to be lopped, sometimes by aphaeresis, more often by apocope,
in order to fit the metrical bed, but also rack them out by lengthening
of vowels, gemination of consonants, double prepositions (e.g. pavalitta
$\sim$ {\em pradi1pta}) or adding meaningless suffixes like
(a/i)ga/ya or, rarely it seems, -n2a (a case in point of which may be
osavan2a $\sim$ {\em utsava}). For them I can refer to, e.g., Mme Balbir's
recent study (Balbir 1993: 52 note 71). As to the diction as such it may be
remarked that more than once we meet with Pra1krit words the Sanskrit
equivalent of which is attested for the Bha1gavatapura1n2a only, as e.g.,
urvarita (621, 2851), gobbara (1731, 1734) and parokkha (214, 565).
PSM mentions other rare BKBh words as occurring in the Supa1sana1hacariya
which therefore may be worth a systematic evaluation. In many cases I
did not find a satisfying etymon, not only as regards {\em des4i1}
words, but also where the commentary explains one rare word by another
as, e.g., at ujjhin2iga1 and Sa. 4pa1ris2t2ha1panika15, vuvajjiya and Sa.
{\em vya1yita}.\\
The Glossary includes also words from Pra1krit passages in the commentary
({\em uda1karan2a}s etc.).\\

At the occasion of his eightiest birthday this volume is  dedicated to
Professor Harivallabh Bha1ya1n2i1 whom I never in vain consulted.\\

a-ghun2iya {\em a + ghun2a + -ika/-ita} 1957 (cty: {\em gun2air a-viddha})\\
a-ciyatta  {\em a + tyakta} (Bolle4e 1994a: 207; if this etymology is correct, {\em tyakta} may here have the active meaning of `having left out, omitted;not having done', viz., cooking especially for a monk or nun. The {\em locus classicus} may be  Dasav 5,1,17 a-ciyattam2 na pavise, ciyattam2 pavise kulam2 `he shouldnot go to a family that did cook especially for him' -- food which he wasnot allowed to accept and for which reason he could consider those people "unkind" to him --, `but he should go where one had abstained therefrom' so as to enable him to take what was offered as being pure. Haribhadra comments this passage by {\em a-pri1ti-kulam2 yatra pravis4adbhih2 sa1dhubhir a-pri1tir utpadyate na ca niva1rayanti kutas4cin nimitta7ntara1t. Etad api na pravis4et tat-sam2kle4sa-nimittatva-prasan3ga1t. Ciyattam [...] pravis4et kulam2 tad-anugraha-prasan3ga1t}. Perhaps a-ciyatta, too, should thus be listed among the negative expressions of the {\em a-him2sa1} type. Charpentier's difficulty [1922: 320] is partly due to his wrong reading a-vyatta at Utt 11, 9. As ciyatta is rare compared to its negative one may assume that many people tried to improve their {\em karman} by meansnot sanctioned by the monastic rules); -- 1264 (cty: {\em a-pri1tika}), 1268, 1394, 1489, 1579-81, 3707, 5242 etc.\\

a-sai11  {\em a + sati}  loc. sg. masc. of {\em sat}  `in the absence of' (c. gen.). Cf. Schubring 1963: 236; Bolle4e 1994a: 132; frequently as a-sai1 ya (often written as -e) for which expletive addition cf. chac ca\ ?
